<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Footprints of parasitism in the genome of the parasitic flowering plant Cuscuta campestris"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2018 9:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-06-28"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="A parasitic lifestyle, where plants procure some or all of their nutrients from other living plants, has evolved independently in many dicotyledonous plant families and is a major threat for agriculture globally. Nevertheless, no genome sequence of a parasitic plant has been reported to date. Here we describe the genome sequence of the parasitic field dodder, Cuscuta campestris. The genome contains signatures of a fairly recent whole-genome duplication and lacks genes for pathways superfluous to a parasitic lifestyle. Specifically, genes needed for high photosynthetic activity are lost, explaining the low photosynthesis rates displayed by the parasite. Moreover, several genes involved in nutrient uptake processes from the soil are lost. On the other hand, evidence for horizontal gene transfer by way of genomic DNA integration from the parasite&#8217;s hosts is found. We conclude that the parasitic lifestyle has left characteristic footprints in the C. campestris genome. Parasitic lifestyles leave unique genomic footprints. Here, the authors describe the genome sequence of a parasitic plant, Cuscuta campestris, and find that gene losses and host gene acquisitions reflect the independence from photosynthesis and the ability to retain and express chunks of foreign genomic DNA."/>

data-author-popup="auth-Karsten-Fischer">Karsten Fischer</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff3">3</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="UiT The Arctic University of Norway, Biologibygget" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000000122595234, grid.10919.30, Department of Arctic and Marine Biology, UiT The Arctic University of Norway, Biologibygget, Framstredet 39, Tromsø, 9037, Norway" /></span></sup><sup class="u-js-hide"> <a href="#na1">na1</a></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Anthony-Bolger" data-author-popup="auth-Anthony-Bolger">Anthony Bolger</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="RWTH Aachen             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>A parasitic lifestyle, where plants procure some or all of their nutrients from other living plants, has evolved independently in many dicotyledonous plant families and is a major threat for agriculture globally. Nevertheless, no genome sequence of a parasitic plant has been reported to date. Here we describe the genome sequence of the parasitic field dodder, <i>Cuscuta campestris</i>. The genome contains signatures of a fairly recent whole-genome duplication and lacks genes for pathways superfluous to a parasitic lifestyle. Specifically, genes needed for high photosynthetic activity are lost, explaining the low photosynthesis rates displayed by the parasite. Moreover, several genes involved in nutrient uptake processes from the soil are lost. On the other hand, evidence for horizontal gene transfer by way of genomic DNA integration from the parasite’s hosts is found. We conclude that the parasitic lifestyle has left characteristic footprints in the <i>C. campestris</i> genome.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The field dodder (<i>Cuscuta campestris</i> Yunck.) belongs to the genus <i>Cuscuta</i> (Convolvulaceae, Solanales) that comprises about 200 parasitic plant species, many of which are devastating agronomic weeds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Dawson, J. H., Musselman, L. I., Wolswinkel, P. &amp; Dörr, I. Biology and control of Cuscuta. Rev. Weed Sci. 6, 265–317 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d35684e603">1</a></sup>. As a result of their parasitic nature, which consists of winding around and infecting the aerial parts of their host plants, they have a severely altered morphology: they do not possess roots or proper leaves (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig1">1</a>a, b), but instead have specialized infection organs for host attachment and intrusion called haustoria. The haustoria grow into the host tissue and connect the vascular systems of the two plants (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig1">1</a>c), thus enabling the parasite to absorb water, photoassimilates, and other organic and inorganic compounds from its host. In contrast to most other parasitic plants and to plant pathogens in general, dodder species, including <i>C. campestris</i>, have a remarkably broad host range, which includes annuals, shrubs, and trees representing different lineages across the plant kingdom (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig1">1</a>d)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dawson, J. H., Musselman, L. I., Wolswinkel, P. &amp; Dörr, I. Biology and control of Cuscuta. Rev. Weed Sci. 6, 265–317 (1994)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d35684e620">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Costea, M. &amp; Tardif, F. J. The biology of canadian weeds. 133. Cuscuta campestris Yuncker, C. gronovii Willd. ex Schult., C. umbrosa Beyr. ex Hook., C. epithymum (L.) and C. epilinum Weihe. Can. J. Plant Sci. 86, 293–316 (2005)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d35684e620_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Cooke, D., &amp; Black, I. Biology and control of Cuscuta campestris and other Cuscuta spp.: a bibliographic review. Technical Paper, Department of Agriculture, South Australia 18, 33 (1987)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d35684e623">3</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04344-z/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4344_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04344-z/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4344_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="483" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><i>Cuscuta campestris</i>—host plant relationship. <b>a</b> <i>Cuscuta campestris</i> (yellow) infecting <i>Pelargonium zonale</i>. <b>b</b> Close-up of a parasitic vine entwining and infecting a host shoot. <b>c</b> Cross section through an infection site. The right half of the micrograph was overlayed with color to emphasize the border between <i>C. campestris</i> (yellow) and the <i>P</i>. <i>zonale</i> stem (green). <b>d</b> Simplified phylogenetic tree of reported host plant species of <i>C. campestris</i>, showing the variety of angiosperm orders (named on the right) that contain host species (according to refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dawson, J. H., Musselman, L. I., Wolswinkel, P. &amp; Dörr, I. Biology and control of Cuscuta. Rev. Weed Sci. 6, 265–317 (1994)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d35684e670">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Costea, M. &amp; Tardif, F. J. The biology of canadian weeds. 133. Cuscuta campestris Yuncker, C. gronovii Willd. ex Schult., C. umbrosa Beyr. ex Hook., C. epithymum (L.) and C. epilinum Weihe. Can. J. Plant Sci. 86, 293–316 (2005)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d35684e670_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Cooke, D., &amp; Black, I. Biology and control of Cuscuta campestris and other Cuscuta spp.: a bibliographic review. Technical Paper, Department of Agriculture, South Australia 18, 33 (1987)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d35684e673">3</a></sup>). The gray background highlights the single described monocot host. The asterisk indicates the closest phylogenetic relative of <i>C. campestris</i> (Convolvulaceae) within the host groups</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>For several <i>Cuscuta</i> species, reductions in the size and coding potential of the plastid genomes have been reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Funk, H. T., Berg, S., Krupinska, K., Maier, U. G. &amp; Krause, K. Complete DNA sequences of the plastid genomes of two parasitic flowering plant species, Cuscuta reflexa and Cuscuta gronovii. BMC Plant Biol. 7, 45 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d35684e695">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="McNeal, J. R., Kuehl, J. V., Boore, J. L. &amp; dePamphilis, C. W. Complete plastid genome sequences suggest strong selection for retention of photosynthetic genes in the parasitic plant genus Cuscuta. BMC Plant Biol. 7, 57 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d35684e698">5</a></sup>. Unfortunately, aside from several recent transcriptome analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Honaas, L. A. et al. Functional genomics of a generalist parasitic plant: laser microdissection of host-parasite interface reveals host-specific patterns of parasite gene expression. BMC Plant Biol. 13, 9 (2013)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d35684e702">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kim, G., LeBlanc, M. L., Wafula, E. K., dePamphilis, C. W. &amp; Westwood, J. H. Plant science. Genomic-scale exchange of mRNA between a parasitic plant and its hosts. Science 345, 808–811 (2014)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d35684e702_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ranjan, A. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the transcriptome of the parasitic weed Cuscuta pentagona identifies genes associated with plant parasitism. Plant Physiol. 166, 1186–1199 (2014)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d35684e702_2">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Olsen, S. et al. Getting ready for host invasion: elevated expression and action of xyloglucan endotransglucosylases/hydrolases in developing haustoria of the holoparasitic angiosperm Cuscuta. J. Exp. Bot. 67, 695–708 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d35684e705">9</a></sup> there is very little information on the other cellular genomes of these shoot parasites. To an even larger extent, the phenomenon of plastid genome downsizing is also visible in root-parasitizing Orobanchaceae that cannot support themselves by photosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Wicke, S. in Parasitic Orobanchaceae (eds Joel, D. M., Gressel, J. &amp; Musselman, L. J.) Ch. 15, 267–286 (Springer, Berlin Heidelberg 2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d35684e709">10</a></sup>. In contrast, in the latter group some nuclear genomes appear to have increased in size and potentially in function<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Wicke, S. in Parasitic Orobanchaceae (eds Joel, D. M., Gressel, J. &amp; Musselman, L. J.) Ch. 15, 267–286 (Springer, Berlin Heidelberg 2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d35684e713">10</a></sup>. Still, a full nuclear genome sequence has not been published for any parasitic plant to date.</p><p>To close that knowledge gap, we describe here the genome of <i>C. campestris</i> as derived from shotgun sequencing, genome assembly, gene annotation, and repetitive element analysis. This genome bears footprints of <i>Cuscuta’s</i> parasitic lifestyle in the form of horizontally acquired genetic material from the host and in the form of gene losses that are in agreement with the differences in their physiology, compared to non-parasitic plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Karyology and genome characteristics</h3><p><i>C. campestris</i> possesses 2<i>n</i> = 56 small chromosomes as revealed by Feulgen staining and has a 1C-value of 581 megabase pairs (Mbp; or 0.594 when given in pg) based on our flow cytometric measurements. Chromosome sizes range from 0.8 to 1.5 µm, with only a few (10–12) larger chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig2">2a</a>). Whole-genome shotgun sequencing was performed using Illumina paired-end data coupled with Illumina mate pair libraries of different insert sizes and PacBio long reads for scaffolding. This provided a size of just under 477 Mbp for the filtered and assembled draft genome sequence, representing about 86% of the k-mer-based genome size estimate of 556 Mbp and 82% of the 581 Mbp size indicated by flow cytometry. It contains 6907 scaffolds with sizes up to 5,342,339 bp (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">1</a>), featuring an N<sub>50</sub> (minimum scaffold length at 50% of the genome) of 1.38 Mbp. The genome contains 44,303 predicted high confidence gene loci, excluding splice variants, that were supported either by transcriptome data from this parasite and/or sequence similarity to known sequences. A BUSCO analysis to assess gene completeness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d35684e751">11</a></sup> indicated, that of 1440 genes typically conserved in plants, 82.1% were present in the assembly, and that 59% of these 1440 genes were present in a duplicated form (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04344-z/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4344_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04344-z/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4344_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="331" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Genome features. <b>a</b> Feulgen-stained metaphase chromosomes of <i>C. campestris</i> (2<i>n</i> = 56). The scale bar represents 10 µm. <b>b</b> Insertion age distribution of <i>C. campestris</i> full-length LTR retrotransposons in comparison to tomato (<i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>) as another Solanales representative. Insertion events of long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons were dated by comparing the divergence between the 5′ and 3′ long terminal repeats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="SanMiguel, P., Gaut, B. S., Tikhonov, A., Nakajima, Y. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. The paleontology of intergene retrotransposons of maize. Nat. Genet. 20, 43–45 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d35684e788">61</a></sup>. A random mutation rate of 7*10<sup>−9</sup> was used to convert the sequence divergence into age<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Ossowski, S. et al. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 327, 92–94 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d35684e794">62</a></sup> (in million years)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Of the assembled genome sequence, 46.2% (221 Mbp) was identified as transposon derived with a strong dominance of long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons (96% of all mobile genetic elements). The amount and composition of the repetitive genome fraction (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">3</a>) is similar to what was found in solanaceous plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d35684e812">12</a></sup> and lends support to a good assembly, capturing the repetitive content that is expected for the genome size. Interestingly, the insertion age distribution of all full-length LTR retrotransposons and their superfamily subgroups shows in comparison to other Solanales, like tomato, a more recent amplification wave around 1.5 million years ago (mya) with only minor differences between their subgroups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig2">2b</a>) which could have been triggered by a polyploidisation event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Petit, M. et al. Mobilization of retrotransposons in synthetic allotetraploid tobacco. New Phytol. 186, 135–147 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d35684e819">13</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Whole-genome duplication</h3><p>An orthogroup analysis was performed between <i>C. campestris</i>, the related species <i>Ipomoea nil</i> and ten other species including Rosids, Asterids, and more distant Monocots (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM3">1</a>). This revealed that <i>C. campestris</i> indeed has duplicate copies of many genes when compared to other species, including, but not limited to, representatives of the Solanales (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig3">3a</a>). The peak synonymous substitution rate (d<i>S</i>) was on average much lower between paralogs in <i>C. campestris</i> (0.05), than between genes of the parasite and their orthologs in <i>I. nil</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hoshino, A. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the Japanese morning glory Ipomoea nil. Nat. Commun. 7, 13295 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d35684e855">14</a></sup> (0.5, split about 55 mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Eserman, L. A., Tiley, G. P., Jarret, R. L., Leebens-Mack, J. H. &amp; Miller, R. E. Phylogenetics and diversification of morning glories (tribe Ipomoeeae, Convolvulaceae) based on whole plastome sequences. Am. J. Bot. 101, 92–103 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d35684e859">15</a></sup>) and <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (1.2, split &gt; 80 mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Eserman, L. A., Tiley, G. P., Jarret, R. L., Leebens-Mack, J. H. &amp; Miller, R. E. Phylogenetics and diversification of morning glories (tribe Ipomoeeae, Convolvulaceae) based on whole plastome sequences. Am. J. Bot. 101, 92–103 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d35684e867">15</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Bremer, K., Friis, E. M. &amp; Bremer, B. Molecular phylogenetic dating of asterid flowering plants shows early Cretaceous diversification. Syst. Biol. 53, 496–505 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d35684e870">16</a></sup>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig3">3b</a>). The majority of genes in <i>C. campestris</i> (59% compared to 23% in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>) had a syntenic paralog, and paralogs with a d<i>S</i> value around the peak at 0.05 were especially likely to be syntenic (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">1</a>). Further, the most common ratio between genes in syntenic blocks was two homologs in <i>C. campestris</i> to one in <i>S. lycopersicum</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">2</a>). Functional enrichment analysis in orthogroups with twice or more the genes in <i>C. campestris</i> than in other dicot species showed, furthermore, that tendencies towards conserving duplicated genes are particularly conspicuous in MapMan categories including “DNA” and “protein” functions. Taken together, the high proportion of gene duplicates featuring a low d<i>S</i> value and the recent LTR-retrotransposon proliferation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig2">2b</a>) point to a recent genome duplication event.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04344-z/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4344_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04344-z/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4344_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="325" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Duplication and loss of genes in the <i>C. campestris</i> genome. <b>a</b> Overview over 14,626 asterid orthogroups from <i>C. campestris</i>, <i>Ipomoea nil</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>Mimulus guttatus,</i> and <i>Daucus carota</i>. The Venn diagram shows the number of overlapping and exclusive orthogroups found in the five species. The bar diagram below summarizes gene content in orthogroups in <i>C. campestris</i> and eight divergent dicot species. Indicated in black are orthogroups with exactly one member in each of these species (202 OGs). Orthogroups missing in the respective species only, but that are otherwise present in all species once (exactly 0:1, named:other species, striped) or more than once (ratio 0:1 or more, solid color) are shown in orange and are underlined in the Venn diagram. Duplicated orthogroups are defined as having exactly 2:1 genes per species (striped purple) or as having a ratio of at least 2n:n with at least one gene in every species (solid purple). All above ratios are (named species):(other species). <b>b</b> Synonymous substitution rate (d<i>S</i>) analysis. Density of d<i>S</i> rates for <i>C. campestris</i> paralogs are shown in purple, and for 1:1 orthologs with <i>I. nil</i> and <i>S. lycopersicum</i> in green and blue, respectively. <b>c</b> Functional enrichment analysis of lost and duplicated genes. MapMan categories for genes that are significantly enriched are depicted using the logarithmic values of their <i>p</i> values. The orange bars represent lost genes that are present in one or more copies in the eight other dicot species shown in <b>a</b> but are lost in <i>C. campestris</i> (ratio 0:1 or more). Similarly, purple bars represent categories significantly enriched for duplicated genes (at least 2<i>n</i>:<i>n</i>)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene loss</h3><p>To uncover further manifestations of the parasitic lifestyle in the genome in the form of gene or pathway losses, 1027 gene families that lack an ortholog in <i>C. campestris</i> but are otherwise conserved in all other tested dicots (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig3">3a</a>) were subjected to a functional enrichment analysis on the basis of MapMan categories<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Usadel, B. et al. Extension of the visualization tool MapMan to allow statistical analysis of arrays, display of corresponding genes, and comparison with known responses. Plant Physiol. 138, 1195–1204 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1004">17</a></sup>. Functional enrichments were particularly observed for the categories “photosynthesis”, “RNA”, “stress”, “transport”, and “lipid metabolism” (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig3">3c</a>), which contrasts with the categories enriched for duplications.</p><p>In a second, independent approach, 1736 gene losses were found in <i>C. campestris</i> relative to <i>I. nil</i> when performing one-to-one blast searches with the ~23,700 conserved proteins of this closest available non-parasitic relative of <i>Cuscuta</i>. In total, 42 random proteins from a broad cross section of functional categories that were contained in both lists, and that were of particular interest due to their involvement in processes that distinguish <i>C. campestris</i> from non-parasitic plants, were manually checked for their conservation across the plant kingdom (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig4">4a</a>). With the exception of some genes involved in symbiotic interactions that are lacking in Brassicales and Caryophyllales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Delaux, P. M. et al. Comparative phylogenomics uncovers the impact of symbiotic associations on host genome evolution. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004487 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1030">18</a></sup>, but are present in all other plant lineages, these genes are conserved across land plants, supporting the assumption that their function has become obsolete due to the parasitic lifestyle. While <i>C. campestris</i> has lost genes that are needed for photosynthesis at higher light intensities (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig4">4b, c</a>), the genes for the photosynthetic light reactions (linear and one of the two cyclic electron transport pathways) and complete chlorophyll and carotenoid synthesis pathways were retained. Similarly, all amino acid and fatty acid synthesis genes, as well as co-enzyme and vitamin pathway genes are present, although the parasite is thought to obtain all these compounds from its hosts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Aly, R. Trafficking of molecules between parasitic plants and their hosts. Weed Res. 53, 231–241 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1040">19</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04344-z/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4344_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04344-z/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4344_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="619" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Comparison of gene losses between <i>C. campestris</i> and autotrophic plants. <b>a</b> Presence (gray) and absence (red) of 42 lost genes connected to photosynthesis, metabolism, transport processes, and symbiotic interactions in 19 sequenced genomes (from Phytozome v.12.0) of the angiosperms, lower plants, and green algae. Species names are shown on the right in the order of increasing evolutionary distance from <i>C. campestris</i> (from top to bottom). Gene abbreviations and MapMan bincodes are given on the top. Full gene names and descriptions of the bincodes are given in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">5</a>. Asterisks behind species names indicate that the sequences from these species were used as query for manual homology searches against the <i>C. campestris</i> genome. <b>b</b> Schematic view of primary carbon metabolism, i.e., Calvin cycle, photorespiration, plastid and cytosolic glycolysis and gluconeogenesis, and synthesis of the compatible solutes mannitol, raffinose and stachyose. Enzymatic reactions for which genes are missing are indicated by circled red crosses. E4P erythrose-4-phosphate, F6P fructose-6-phosphate, FBP fructose-1,6-bisphosphate, G1P glucose-1-phosphate, G6P glucose-6-phosphate, Glyc glycerate, Gly glycine, Gl glycolate, Gl2P glycolate-2-phosphate, Glyox glyoxylate, HyPyr hydroxypyruvate, Ino inositol, M6P mannose-6-phosphate, PEP phosphoenolpyruvate, PGA 3-phosphoglycerate, BPGA 1,3-bisphosphoglycerate, Pyr pyruvate, Raf raffinose, R5P ribose-5-phosphate, Ru5P ribulose-5-phosphate, RuBP ribulose-1,5-bisphosphate, S7P sedulose-7-phosphate, SBP sedulose-1,7-bisphosphate, Ser serine, Sta stachyose, Suc sucrose, TrioseP triose phosphates, UDP-Gal UDP-galactose, Xu5P xylulose-5-phosphate. <b>c</b> Schematic view of photosynthetic electron transport, chlorophyll regeneration, and degradation pathways and phylloquinone synthesis. Enzymatic reactions for which genes are missing are indicated by circled red crosses. Chl a chlorophyll a, Chlide <i>a</i> Chlorophyllide <i>a</i>, Cyt<i>b</i><sub>
                            <i>6</i>
                          </sub><i>f</i> cytochrome <i>b</i><sub>
                            <i>6</i>
                          </sub><i>f</i> complex, D1 D1 protein of photosystem II, D1* damaged D1 protein, DePhyQ demethylphylloquinone, e<sup>−</sup> electron, Fd ferredoxin, Glu glutamate, NDH NADH-dehydrogenase complex, PC plastocyanin, PGR5/PGRL1 proton gradient regulation 5/PGR5-like photosynthetic phenotype 1-complex, Pheide <i>a</i> pheophorbide <i>a</i>, Pheo <i>a</i> pheophytin <i>a</i>, PhyQ phylloquinone, PpIX Protoporphyrin IX, PQ plastoquinone, PS I photosystem I, PS II photosystem II, PTOX plastid terminal oxidase</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Horizontal acquisition of genes</h3><p>It has been shown that root-parasitic Orobanchaceae acquired a number of genes by horizontal gene transfer (HGT) from their hosts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010–E7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1134">20</a></sup>. Similarly, two incidents of HGT have been reported on the basis of transcriptome studies in <i>Cuscuta pentagona</i> and <i>Cuscuta australis</i>. One example is an albumin gene acquired from legumes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Evolution of a horizontally acquired legume gene, albumin 1, in the parasitic plant Phelipanche aegyptiaca and related species. BMC Evol. Biol. 13, 48 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1144">21</a></sup> and the other a strictosidine synthase-like gene originating from the Brassicaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Zhang, D. et al. Root parasitic plant Orobanche aegyptiaca and shoot parasitic plant Cuscuta australis obtained Brassicaceae-specific strictosidine synthase-like genes by horizontal gene transfer. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 19 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1148">22</a></sup>. In both cases, the presence of introns in contigs or genomic PCR amplicons suggested that these integrations occurred via DNA uptake. Large-scale transport of phloem-mobile transcripts appears to occur in both directions across the host–parasite border, tentatively suggesting that an integration mechanism via RNA or cDNA is also possible<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kim, G., LeBlanc, M. L., Wafula, E. K., dePamphilis, C. W. &amp; Westwood, J. H. Plant science. Genomic-scale exchange of mRNA between a parasitic plant and its hosts. Science 345, 808–811 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1153">7</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Yoshida, S., Maruyama, S., Nozaki, H. &amp; Shirasu, K. Horizontal gene transfer by the parasitic plant Striga hermonthica. Science 328, 1128 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1156">23</a></sup>. So far, however, horizontal DNA transfer in <i>Cuscuta</i> beyond these cases of individual genes has not been analyzed. In search for horizontally acquired genes in the <i>C. campestris</i> genome, we identified, in addition to the above mentioned horizontally transferred genes, 64 novel high confidence HGT candidates from at least 32 different donor sequences that show a functional bias towards defence reactions or have unknown functions in their hosts (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">4</a>). All of them group phylogenetically with orders other than the Solanales (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig5">5a, b</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">4</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">4</a>), where the genus <i>Cuscuta</i> is phylogenetically placed (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">5</a>). A majority of these events can be traced back to the preferred host orders Fabales and Caryophyllales suggestive of HGT between host and parasite (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig5">5a, b</a>). To refute that these putative HGT events are a result of gene duplication or other phenomena, the Notung software package was used to infer models that best explain the disagreement between species trees and gene trees<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Stolzer, M. et al. Inferring duplications, losses, transfers and incomplete lineage sorting with nonbinary species trees. Bioinformatics 28, i409–i415 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1191">24</a></sup>. For all HGT candidates, the Notung models confirm acquisition by HGT (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">7</a>). This conclusion is further supported by the observation that a small part of the transposable element (TE) complement was identified via non-<i>Cuscuta</i> repeat library sequences, often from soybean and <i>Medicago truncatula</i>. Intriguingly, two <i>C. campestris</i> genes that are adjacent to each other show highest homology to two neighboring genes from the <i>Daucus carota</i> (carrot) genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 48, 657–666 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1218">25</a></sup>, <i>Cc021598</i>, and <i>Cc021599</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig5">5c</a>). In the vicinity of these conserved genes are remnants of further genes that were not fully retained after transfer. Moreover, the two introns in the gene <i>Cc021599</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig5">5c</a>) are located in the same position as in its ortholog from carrot (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">8</a>). In addition, this gene appears to have undergone duplication in <i>C. campestris</i> or its recent ancestor. The duplicate sequence, <i>Cc021601</i> is 85% identical to <i>Cc021599</i> (pairwise identity of the gene sequence, CDS pairwise identity is 93.9%) and both of them have a high homology (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig5">5c</a>) to the same carrot gene (with just under 50% pairwise identity for the CDS).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04344-z/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4344_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-04344-z/MediaObjects/41467_2018_4344_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="293" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Horizontal gene transfer (HGT) in <i>C. campestris</i>. <b>a</b> Depiction of the phylogenetic origin of 36 HGT events and their functional category assignment. Phylogenetic orders from which HGT originated are color-coded according to their evolutionary distance to <i>Cuscuta</i> using the median values in million years (MYA) for the split as predicted by Timetree (<a href="http://timetree.org">http://timetree.org</a>) and supported by a species tree calculated using Astral-II (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">5</a>). White numbers refer to the number of genes per donor order, black numbers to the right indicate the number of genes in the recipient, <i>C. campestris</i>. Cake diagrams represent functional assignment of donor genes in MapMan bins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Usadel, B. et al. Extension of the visualization tool MapMan to allow statistical analysis of arrays, display of corresponding genes, and comparison with known responses. Plant Physiol. 138, 1195–1204 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1287">17</a></sup>. <b>b</b> Phylogenetic placement of HGT candidate protein Cc027215, a G-type lectin S-receptor-like serine/threonine-protein kinase, Callus Expression of RBCS 101 (CES101). The amino acid sequence of Cc027215 (highlighted in yellow) is nested deeply within a cluster of orthologous proteins from the hypothetical donor order Fabales (in red), while all orthologs from Solanales species (in gray), that are much more closely related to <i>C. campestris</i>, are located on the second branch. The schematic sequence comparison shows identities relative to Cc027215 in black boxes, discrepancies in gray boxes and gaps in the alignment as black lines. Bootstrap values after 1000 replicates were 86% or higher for all nodes. <b>c</b> Nucleotide sequence comparison of a 36 kb region of chromosome 3 from <i>Daucus carota</i> (top) and a region of <i>C. campestris</i> scaffold123. Coding sequences are shown in dark green and introns as well as up- and down-stream untranslated flanking regions are shown in light green. Areas with very high similarity, indicating conserved remnants of a previous horizontal DNA transfer between both species are indicated by gray fields. Within the depicted area, a gene duplication (<i>Cc021601</i> and <i>Cc021599</i> both mapping with all three exons and two introns to <i>DCAR_012700</i>), a larger sequence insertion between <i>Cc021599</i> and <i>Cc021598</i>, as well as sequence inversions can be seen</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Reads representing many of the HGT candidates were found in one or more RNAseq samples that were used to support gene calling (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM4">2</a>), indicating that the transferred genes are expressed. Some of them, moreover, showed strong up- or downregulation in haustorial vs. control tissue (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">9</a>), suggesting that their gene products may play a role during the infection process.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>Genome sizes and chromosome numbers vary considerably between different <i>Cuscuta</i> species (1C = 470 Mbp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Kubesova, M., Moravcova, L., Suda, J., Jarosik, V. &amp; Pysek, P. Naturalized plants have smaller genomes than their non-invading relatives: a flow cytometric analysis of the Czech alien flora. Preslia 82, 81–96 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1351">26</a></sup>–32 Gbp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="McNeal, J. R., Arumugunathan, K., Kuehl, J. V., Boore, J. L. &amp; dePamphilis, C. W. Systematics and plastid genome evolution of the cryptically photosynthetic parasitic plant genus Cuscuta (Convolvulaceae). BMC Biol. 5, 55 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1355">27</a></sup>), and occasionally different sizes based on flow cytometry have even been reported for the same species (e.g., refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Kubesova, M., Moravcova, L., Suda, J., Jarosik, V. &amp; Pysek, P. Naturalized plants have smaller genomes than their non-invading relatives: a flow cytometric analysis of the Czech alien flora. Preslia 82, 81–96 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1359">26</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="McNeal, J. R., Arumugunathan, K., Kuehl, J. V., Boore, J. L. &amp; dePamphilis, C. W. Systematics and plastid genome evolution of the cryptically photosynthetic parasitic plant genus Cuscuta (Convolvulaceae). BMC Biol. 5, 55 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1362">27</a></sup> for <i>C. campestris</i>). The 1C value of 581 Mbp that we determined for <i>C. campestris</i> is within this range at its lower end. <i>C. campestris</i> reveals signs of a whole-genome duplication around 1.5 mya (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig3">3</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><p>It has often been suggested and experimentally verified for some systems that the tight epigenetic control of transposons is impaired after polyploidisation events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Yaakov, B. &amp; Kashkush, K. Methylation, transcription, and rearrangements of transposable elements in synthetic allopolyploids. Int. J. Plant Genom. 2011, 569826 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1388">28</a></sup>. In line with this, the insertion age distribution in <i>C. campestris</i>, and—with it—LTR-retrotransposon activity, displays two distinct peaks. The younger more prominent event occurred around 1.5 mya where the majority of LTR insertions originated and that affected all subgroups likewise (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig2">2b</a>). In addition, the remnants of an older amplification wave around 5–6 mya ago is visible. In contrast, overall LTR-retrotransposon activity in tomato only peaks between 3.5 and 6.5 mya and only some Ty1/<i>copia</i> elements seem younger. That the younger peak coincides with a recent genome duplication event was also inferred from substitution rate and synteny analyses as well as a BUSCO analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig3">3</a>, Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">2</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><p>Besides finding 59% duplicated genes in a set of 1440 conserved genes, BUSCO yielded higher counts for missing genes than with the “classical” genomes of related photosynthetic plants (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">2</a>). Further analyses revealed that exhaustive gene losses have occurred in <i>C. campestris</i>. The gene classes that were indicated to be lost by the BUSCO analysis coincide with those identified by other means (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig3">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig4">4</a>), explaining, at least in part, the poorer performance of the software in this respect.</p><p>Many of these nuclear-encoded losses in the parasite were found to be associated with one of three categories: redundant pathways or redundant functions, high photosynthetic production rates, and the interaction with the biotic and abiotic environment (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig3">3</a>c, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig4">4</a>). For instance, genes for the NDH-dependent cyclic electron transport pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Suorsa, M., Sirpio, S. &amp; Aro, E. M. Towards characterization of the chloroplast NAD(P)H dehydrogenase complex. Mol. Plant 2, 1127–1140 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1438">29</a></sup> and genes for a dephytylase and a dechelatase needed for chlorophyll turnover<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lin, Y. P., Wu, M. C. &amp; Charng, Y. Y. Identification of a chlorophyll dephytylase involved in chlorophyll turnover in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 28, 2974–2990 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1442">30</a></sup> are important to adjust photosynthesis in situations of high fixation rates and fluctuating light conditions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig4">4</a>). They are apparently superfluous for <i>C. campestris</i>, where photosynthesis rates are so low that the CO<sub>2</sub> compensation point is never reached<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="van der Kooij, T. A. W., Krause, K., Doerr, I. &amp; Krupinska, K. Molecular, functional and ultrastructural characterisation of plastids from six species of the parasitic flowering plant genus Cuscuta. Planta 210, 701–707 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1455">31</a></sup>. This is in line with the retention of the photosynthesis genes encoded on the plastid genome (with few exceptions)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Funk, H. T., Berg, S., Krupinska, K., Maier, U. G. &amp; Krause, K. Complete DNA sequences of the plastid genomes of two parasitic flowering plant species, Cuscuta reflexa and Cuscuta gronovii. BMC Plant Biol. 7, 45 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1459">4</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="McNeal, J. R., Kuehl, J. V., Boore, J. L. &amp; dePamphilis, C. W. Complete plastid genome sequences suggest strong selection for retention of photosynthetic genes in the parasitic plant genus Cuscuta. BMC Plant Biol. 7, 57 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1462">5</a></sup> (see also Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">10</a>) and imposes the question why these pathways are still complete in the parasite even though they appear to be too inefficient to enable host-independent growth. Developing seeds of autotrophic plants, many of which are green during embryogenesis and show some photosynthetic activity despite depending heavily on imported nutrients, are in an analogous situation. Interestingly, it has been shown that re-fixation of CO<sub>2</sub> released during fatty acid biosynthesis, mitochondrial respiration and other processes significantly improves carbon efficiency of seeds and their growth rate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Goffman, F. D., Alonso, A. P., Schwender, J., Shachar-Hill, Y. &amp; Ohlrogge, J. B. Light enables a very high efficiency of carbon storage in developing embryos of rapeseed. Plant Physiol. 138, 2269–2279 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1472">32</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Schwender, J., Goffman, F., Ohlrogge, J. B. &amp; Shachar-Hill, Y. Rubisco without the Calvin cycle improves the carbon efficiency of developing green seeds. Nature 432, 779–782 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1475">33</a></sup>. Thus, it is likely that the low photosynthesis levels in field dodder (and other dodder species) primarily facilitate re-fixation of produced CO<sub>2</sub>. This reduces carbon loss and improves the chances of the parasite successfully bridging the distance from one host to the next in situations where susceptible hosts are not located in close proximity (as in natural ecosystems) or during germination before the parasite has established its first feeding connection.</p><p>Examples of gene losses in <i>C. campestris</i> that mediate environmental responses in autotrophic hosts are mostly connected to soil–root interactions and therefore might be connected to the loss of a root system in dodder species. To this end, iron transporters that are responsible for uptake of iron from the soil<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Vert, G. et al. IRT1, an Arabidopsis transporter essential for iron uptake from the soil and for plant growth. Plant Cell 14, 1223–1233 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1488">34</a></sup>, as well as proteins involved in the establishment of symbiotic relationships between plants and mycorrhizal fungi, were found to be dispensable in <i>C. campestris</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig4">4a</a>). Genes belonging to the latter group were previously found missing in Brassicaceae and Caryophyllales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Delaux, P. M. et al. Comparative phylogenomics uncovers the impact of symbiotic associations on host genome evolution. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004487 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1498">18</a></sup> but are present in most other families (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig4">4a</a>). One nitrate uptake system of roots (the high affinity NRT2/NAR2 complex) along with the CEPD peptides regulating its expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Miller, A. J., Fan, X., Orsel, M., Smith, S. J. &amp; Wells, D. M. Nitrate transport and signalling. J. Exp. Bot. 58, 2297–2306 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1506">35</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Ohkubo, Y., Tanaka, M., Tabata, R., Ogawa-Ohnishi, M. &amp; Matsubayashi, Y. Shoot-to-root mobile polypeptides involved in systemic regulation of nitrogen acquisition. Nat. Plants 3, 17029 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1509">36</a></sup> is also missing in <i>C. campestris</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig4">4a</a>). Overall, it appears that typical known traits of the parasite such as its nutritional dependency on the host plant and the ensuing release from the necessity of fixing CO<sub>2</sub> photosynthetically are clearly reflected by changes and gene losses in the genome. This nourishes the hope that, vice versa, gene losses in other than the functional categories described here may also reveal other, more obscure, consequences of parasitism.</p><p>As a result of the close physical connection between parasite and host, the exchange of nucleic acids across species borders is prone to occur. Such events, known as HGT, were already inferred from recent transcriptome analyses for <i>Cuscuta</i> and other parasites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010–E7019 (2016)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1527">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Evolution of a horizontally acquired legume gene, albumin 1, in the parasitic plant Phelipanche aegyptiaca and related species. BMC Evol. Biol. 13, 48 (2013)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1527_1">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, D. et al. Root parasitic plant Orobanche aegyptiaca and shoot parasitic plant Cuscuta australis obtained Brassicaceae-specific strictosidine synthase-like genes by horizontal gene transfer. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 19 (2014)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1527_2">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Yoshida, S., Maruyama, S., Nozaki, H. &amp; Shirasu, K. Horizontal gene transfer by the parasitic plant Striga hermonthica. Science 328, 1128 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1530">23</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Xi, Z. et al. Horizontal transfer of expressed genes in a parasitic flowering plant. BMC Genom. 13, 227 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1533">37</a></sup>, as well as for grafting partners<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Stegemann, S. &amp; Bock, R. Exchange of genetic material between cells in plant tissue grafts. Science 324, 649–651 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1537">38</a></sup>. Although the identification of true HGT events and the bona fide identification of the gene donor can be challenging, the analysis of the <i>C. campestris</i> genome provided unambiguous indications that HGT events have at least in part occurred as a result of an uptake and integration of larger stretches of genomic DNA from hosts that are not closely related to the order Solanales in which <i>Cuscuta</i> is nested (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig5">5</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">5</a>). The exchange of DNA between parasitic plants and their hosts is well known from the analysis of mitochondrial DNA and these exchanges were also shown to preferentially involve bigger pieces of DNA rather than gene-by-gene transfers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Davis, C. C. &amp; Xi, Z. Horizontal gene transfer in parasitic plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 26, 14–19 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1554">39</a></sup>. Alas, examples from the nuclear genome are considerably scarcer. An HGT event in <i>C. campestris</i> that stands out from previous accounts in that it encompasses a larger region on the genome, bears signs of conservation and even gene duplication in some parts and post-transfer changes in others (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig5">5</a>). Whether the conserved and duplicated transferred genes have undergone neo-functionalization to adapt to infection-related tasks or whether their products may help to develop a molecular camouflage, enabling the parasite to remain unnoticed during an attack, remains speculative. The elevated expression of some of them in early developing haustoria provides a first hint at their involvement in the infection process that must be scrutinized further in the future.</p><p>In summary, the <i>C. campestris</i> genome provides clues—hidden in the type and organization of the coding sequences—on evolutionary, physiological and developmental aspects connected to parasitism in plants. This study will pave the way for genome-enabled development of reverse genetic tools to dissect host–parasite interactions as well as genome-guided interpretation of <i>Cuscuta</i> peptidome, secretome, and other sub-proteome data.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Plant material and DNA extraction</h3><p><i>C. campestris</i> was grown in a glasshouse at the phytotron of the University of Tromsø, Norway, in 24 h of light at 21 °C on <i>Pelargonium zonale</i> L. as host. DNA was extracted from stems and flowers using a modified protocol of Peterson et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Peterson, D. G., Boehm, K. S. &amp; Stack, S. M. Isolation of milligram quantities of nuclear DNA from tomato (Lycopersicum esculentum), a plant containing high levels of polyphenolic compounds. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 15, 148–153 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1590">40</a></sup>. In brief, fresh plant material was incubated in the dark at 4 °C for 48 h to remove starch. Afterwards it was homogenized in extraction buffer (2-methyl-2,4-pentanediol (1 M), PIPES (10 mM), MgCl<sub>2</sub> (10 mM), PVP-10 (4% w/v), sodium metabisulfite (10 mM), 2-mercaptoethanol (25 mM), sodium diethyldithiocarbamate (0.5% w/v), <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-lysine (200 mM), and ethylene glycol tetraacetic acid (EGTA (6 mM), pH 6.0). The homogenized material was filtered twice through Miracloth, Triton X-100 was added to a total concentration of 0.5% (v/v) on ice and the sample was incubated for 30 min. Remaining starch was pelleted at 200 × <i>g</i> for 2 min. The supernatant was centrifuged at 800 × <i>g</i> for 20 min at 4 °C and the pellet was resuspended in 50 ml of MPDB (2-methyl-2,4-pentanediol (0.5 M), PIPES-KOH (10 mM), MgCl<sub>2</sub> (10 mM), Triton X-100 (0.5% v/v), sodium metabisulfite (10 mM), 2-mercaptoethanol (5 mM), <span class="u-small-caps">l</span>-lysine (200 mM), and EGTA (6 mM, pH 7.0). Nuclei were pelleted at 650 × <i>g</i> for 20 min at 4 °C and then resuspended in MPDB buffer. Sodium dodecyl sulfate (SDS) was added to a final concentration of 2% (w/v). The sample was gently mixed and incubated at 60 °C for 10 min. Sodium perchlorate was added to final concentration of 1 M. After centrifugation at 400 × <i>g</i> for 20 min at room temperature, the supernatant was extracted twice with an equal volume of phenol/chloroform/isoamyl alcohol (25:24:1). DNA was precipitated by adding sodium acetate (0.3 M, final concentration) and two volumes of absolute ethanol at −20 °C, pelleted and washed with 70% ethanol (v/v).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec10">Genome size estimate</h3><p>Genome size was measured by flow cytometry. Fresh <i>C. campestris</i> plant material was co-chopped together with the internal standard <i>Pisum sativum</i> (1C = 4.42 pg) in isolation buffer (citric acid (0.1 M), Triton X-100 (0.5% w/w), pH 1.5), filtered through a 30 µm nylon mesh, and treated with RNase A (0.15 mg ml<sup>−1</sup>). After staining with propidium iodide (50 mg l<sup>−1</sup> in staining buffer), the suspension was measured with a CyFlow Space flow cytometer (Partec) up to a total of 10,000 particles. The results were calculated according to the formula 1C = (mean object fluorescence intensity/mean standard fluorescence intensity) * 1C of the standard. The results of three preparations were averaged.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Cytology</h3><p>Flower buds and open flowers from one individual plant were fixed in Carnoy’s fixative (ethanol: acetic acid, 3:1) for 24 h at room temperature and stored until use at −20 °C. Material was stained using the standard Feulgen staining technique. Briefly, material was rinsed with tap water, incubated for 20 min in HCl (5N) at room temperature, rinsed and stained with Schiff’s reagent (VWR) for 1 h. Preparations were analyzed with a Zeiss Axioplan microscope, and images were acquired with a digital CCD camera and Axiovision 4.8 software (Carl Zeiss). Chromosome number was established based on analyses of several preparations and at least five intact chromosome spreads.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Genome sequencing</h3><p>Three Illumina TruSeq PCR-free paired-end libraries with insert sizes of 550, 785, and 800 bp were prepared according to the manufacturer’s manual (Part# 15075699 Rev.A). Bead based size selection was applied to the first library, while BluePippin size selection with an internal R2 marker selecting from 700 to 930 bp was used for the two other libraries. DNA input was 4 µg, 8 µg, and 12 µg for libraries 01, 02, and 03, respectively. The remaining steps were conducted according to the Illumina protocol.</p><p>For the 4, 8, and 12 kb insert size Mate-Pair libraries the Nextera Mate Pair Kit (Illumina) was used with modifications. For the 4 kb insert size library two separate reactions with 4 µg of high molecular weight DNA were tagmented according to manufacturer’s instructions. The reactions were cleaned up separately using the Zymo Genomic DNA Clean &amp; Concentrator Kit according to the Illumina Nextera Mate Pair manual and eluted to 30 µl each. The cleaned up tagmented DNA samples were then mixed with 10 µl loading solution (Sage Science), loaded on one lane of a Blue Pippin device (Sage Science) each and size selected from 2 to 6 kb each using the S1 marker (Sage Science). The samples were pooled after Pippin size selection and cleaned up using Ampure XP beads (Beckman Coulter) using an equal ratio of beads and sample. Afterwards strand displacement was carried out using the phi29 DNA polymerase (NEB) according to manufacturer’s instructions followed by another Ampure XP bead clean up with an equal ratio of beads and sample. The rest of the library preparation was carried out according to the Illumina Nextera Mate Pair protocol except using a BioRuptor Pico (Diagenode) for fragmentation with six cycles (5 s on, 60 s off) at 4 °C and a sample volume of 100 µl. The workflow was the same for the 8 kb insert size library except selection from 6 to 10 kb with the BluePippin device (Sage Science).</p><p>For the 12 kb insert size libraries size selection was carried out from 12 to 20 kb. In addition to the changes mentioned above, the tagmentation reaction was also modified to enrich for larger fragments. Instead of 12 µl, 8 µl of the Mate Pair Tagment Enzyme were used with a tagmentation buffer prepared as described earlier<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Tatsumi, K., Nishimura, O., Itomi, K., Tanegashima, C. &amp; Kuraku, S. Optimization and cost-saving in tagmentation-based mate-pair library preparation and sequencing. Biotechniques 58, 253–257 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1657">41</a></sup>. Three separate reactions were used with these volumes and pooled after size selection. Circularization time was extended to 70 h to allow self-ligation of larger fragments.</p><p>For 40 kb mate pair libraries, 15 µg of high molecular weight DNA was sheared using a Covaris g-Tube (cat no. 520079; Covaris) at 3000 rpm for 30 s in an Eppendorf 5415R centrifuge.
