The End-Repair was scaled up according to the NxSeq® 40 kb Mate Pair Cloning Kit protocol (Lucigen). Afterwards a 1× AMPure XP bead cleanup was performed. The end-repaired DNA was size selected using a 0.75% gel cartridge on high-pass protocol for selection of 40,000–80,000 bp on a BluePippin device. The sample was cleaned with 1 volume AMPure XP beads. All subsequent steps were performed according to the manufacturer’s protocol. All above described DNA libraries were sequenced using an Illumina MiSeq sequencer with either 600 cycle V3 (cat. no. MS-102-3003; Illumina) or 150 cycle V3 (cat. no. MS-102-3001; Illumina) chemistry. Illumina RTA version 1.18.54 was used for basecalling and the generation of fastq files with deactivated adapter trimming.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">PacBio sequencing</h3><p>Long read sequencing using the PacBio RS system was performed by the commercial sequencing provider GATC Biotech. Three SMRT cells yielded a total of 493,246 subreads representing 4.16 Gbp of sequence data (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">11</a>). The data with a maximal read length of 48.8 kb and a read length N50 of 12 kb equates to &gt;8-fold genomic coverage. PacBio subreads were deduplicated, by only keeping the longest subread in each case. The resulting, deduplicated set was then used for scaffolding only.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Genome assembly</h3><p>All Illumina data was trimmed for low-quality bases and adapter sequences using Trimmomatic v0.35<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1684">42</a></sup>. Illumina paired-end TruSeq PCR-free libraries were filtered using standard settings, while Mate-pair libraries were further filtered for the transposase sequence. For all libraries quality filtering used a sliding window of 4:15 and a minimal read length of 36 bp.</p><p>Insert sizes were estimated by aligning the filtered reads to the contig assembly as unpaired using bowtie2 (v2.2.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357–359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1691">43</a></sup>. The mapping position of reads aligned to the same contig in the expected orientation was used to calculate the insert size.</p><p>Two fosmid libraries were prepared according to the Lucigen 40 kb Mate Pair cloning kit and sequenced on an Illumina MiSeq sequencer. For the deduplication of fosmid data, the reads were processed with Trimmomatic using standard settings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1698">42</a></sup>. Unpaired reads were discarded and paired reads were subsequently filtered for presence of the “CAC” sequence at the start of both forward and reverse read as well as the “GTAC” restriction enzyme motif. If the motif was found, the read was trimmed accordingly. Only reads with 20 bp or longer were kept. These were aligned to the assembly as unpaired using bowtie2 (v2.2.6) with standard settings. The alignment was used to identify duplicates based on identical mapping positions and discarding ambiguous alignments based on a mapping quality greater than 3. Valid intralinks were checked for correct orientation and used for the insert size estimation.</p><p>For the deduplication of Illumina mate-pair data, all mate-pair libraries were de-duplicated based on the mapping position analogous to the fosmid libraries without initial filtering of the Lucigen fosmid specific “CAC”- and restriction motif.</p><p>Genome characteristics were estimated using a k-mer approach as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311–317 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1709">44</a></sup>. A total of 31 billion 19-mers were created from the 149.11 million filtered paired-end reads using Jellyfish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Marcais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764–770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1713">45</a></sup>. The respective 19-mer depth was plotted to determine the peak k-mer depth of 54. Given the local minimum of the 19-mer depth distribution at a 19-mer depth of 17, a total of 34 million 19-mers were considered to be the result of sequencing errors. Dividing the remaining 30 billion 19-mers by the determined peak 19-mer depth of 54 resulted in a genome size estimate of 557 Mb.</p><p>The trimmed Illumina DNA paired-end data was assembled into contigs using DISCOVAR de novo r52488<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Weisenfeld, N. I. et al. Comprehensive variation discovery in single human genomes. Nat. Genet. 46, 1350–1355 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1720">46</a></sup>. Resulting contigs were used for assembling scaffolds with SSPACE-Standard v3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1724">47</a></sup> using the de-duplicated mate-pair and fosmid libraries. Scaffolds greater than 1000 bp were kept and scaffolded further with PacBio data using SSPACE-Longread v1.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1728">47</a></sup>. At least five links were required in both scaffolding steps for contig pairing. Gaps in the assembly were filled with GapFiller v1.10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Boetzer, M. &amp; Pirovano, W. Toward almost closed genomes with GapFiller. Genome Biol. 13, R56 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1732">48</a></sup> with all Illumina paired-end and mate-pair data but omitting the PacBio data. Subsequently, lowly supported regions on scaffolds were identified and scaffolds split.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Chloroplast genome assembly</h3><p>The chloroplast genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">10</a>) was assembled using a high-coverage subset of the 7.59 M filtered paired-­end reads of the TruSeq PCR­free 800 bp insert library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Marcais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764–770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1747">45</a></sup>. This high coverage subset should be enriched in chloroplast reads, since the chloroplast genome has many copies per cell. To determine which reads came from high coverage regions, the 19-mer coverage of the library was determined and the average 19-­mer coverage was estimated for each read based on the library 19­-mer distribution. Read pairs indicating a 19­-mer coverage greater than 250× were kept and assembled using SPAdes v.3.6.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Bankevich, A. et al. SPAdes: a new genome assembly algorithm and its applications to single-cell sequencing. J. Comput. Biol. 19, 455–477 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1751">49</a></sup> without further error correction. The assembly yielded a total of 29,261 contigs ranging from 128 to 51,323 base pairs. Within the 50 longest contigs, three contigs showed a higher coverage of 266, 382, and 431, respectively, as estimated by SPAdes. Orientation and order of the contigs were built manually by aligning the reads back to the assembly using bwa v0.7.5a­r418<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate long-read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 26, 589–595 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1755">50</a></sup>. For confirmation and to identify the overlapping sequences, all three contigs were searched against each other using BLASTn v2.2.27+ with default settings. Contigs associated to the assembled chloroplast genome were removed from the whole-genome assembly based on alignments using BLAST.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Contaminant removal</h3><p>For the removal of contaminants, the assembly was aligned using a BLAST search against the non-redundant nucleotide NCBI BLAST database. Additionally, all scaffolds were aligned using BLAST against the Enterobacter phage phiX174 reference (NC_001422.1), and the <i>Pelargonium</i> x <i>hortorum</i> chloroplast reference (DQ897681.1). Only the BLAST against <i>Pelargonium</i> indicated full-length hits against 25 small scaffolds that were subsequently excluded from the assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Genome completeness assessment and gene annotation</h3><p>Assessment of genome and gene-set completeness was quantified using BUSCO v3.0.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1784">11</a></sup> using the embryophyta_odb9 single-copy orthologs and default settings. Gene annotation was performed in a multiple-step process. RNAseq data was first used to create draft gene models, which were manually checked, and used to train Augustus. Then RNAseq data and homology data were used as extrinsic evidence to guide de novo gene prediction with Augustus. Finally, predicted genes were post filtered as described below to create a high confidence gene set.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">RNAseq and gene model creation</h3><p>RNAseq was performed on three replicates each of non-infectious feeding stems, detached starving stems and different infection stages to cover all important situations in the parasites life. Samples were sent to GATC (Germany) for sequencing using Illumina MiSeq. Read quality was checked with FastQC v0.11.2 (<a href="http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc">www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc</a>) and trimmed using Trimmomatic, version 0.32<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1803">42</a></sup>. TruSeq3PE-2 adapters and the overrepresented k-mer TATATACTAG, were trimmed allowing three seed mismatches, and a palindrome and simple clip threshold of 30 and 10, respectively. Very low quality (below phred 3) bases were trimmed from both ends of read, and quality trimming was completed with Trimmomatic’s MAXINFO tool with target length of 50 and strictness of 0.8.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Repeat masking</h3><p>Repetitive genomic regions were determined with RepeatMasker using the <i>Arabidopsis</i> repeat element database. Hard masking was used for mapping of RNAseq data and cross-species protein mapping, while soft masking was used during final gene calling and quality control.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Mapping</h3><p>Trimmed RNASeq reads were mapped with TopHat v2.0.14<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1827">51</a></sup> using the very sensitive bowtie2 setting.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Training augustus</h3><p>Cufflinks v2.2.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-Seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1839">52</a></sup> was used to create draft transcript models (settings: min-frags-per-transfrag 100, max-multi-read-fraction 0.5), and Cuffmerge was used to combine the models from each library. Proteins were predicted from these using TRAPID<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Van Bel, M. et al. TRAPID: an efficient online tool for the functional and comparative analysis of de novo RNA-Seq transcriptomes. Genome Biol. 14, R134 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1843">53</a></sup> with orthoCML 5 database as reference, and filtered to those at least 30 amino acids long. From these gene models, a training set was defined of 679 genes (529 to train, 150 to test) that (i) had three-way best BLAST hit between <i>C. campestris</i> draft, <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1852">54</a></sup>, and <i>S. lycopersicum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Tomato Genome Consortium. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635–641 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1859">12</a></sup>; (ii) these three orthologs shared a start codon, and were all within 20 bp in aligned length; (iii) the <i>C. campestris</i> draft genes shared no more than 60% protein sequence homology with another gene in the set; and (iv) start codon of protein was successfully mapped back to genome with Exonerate version 2.2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Slater, G. S. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinform. 6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1866">55</a></sup>. The intergenic region half-way to the next cufflinks gene model was included as genomic background for training Augustus, and training was performed with UTR on.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Providing extrinsic data as hints to augustus</h3><p>Coverage hints for Augustus were generated with rsem-bam2wig (settings: no-fractional-weight<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Li, B. &amp; Dewey, C. N. RSEM: accurate transcript quantification from RNA-Seq data with or without a reference genome. BMC Bioinform. 12, 323 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1878">56</a></sup>) and the Augustus wig2hints script (settings: prune = 0.1, type = exonpart, width = 10, margin = 10, minthresh = 2, minscore = 240, src = W, radius = 4.5). Intron hints were generated with the Augustus bam2hints script (settings: intronsonly).</p><p>Homology hints were obtained by mapping <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, and all entries matching “Cuscuta” from NCBI’s protein database to the genome with Exonerate (settings: model = protein2genome, maxintron = 30000, showtargetgff = T, and percent = 40/50/60 for respective species). Mappings were converted to hints with the Augustus script exonerate2hints.pl (settings: CDSpart_cutoff = 0, and priority <i>Cuscuta</i> &gt; <i>S. lycopersicum</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec23">Gene calling</h3><p>Augustus was configured to heavily weight extrinsic evidence (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM5">3</a>) and run with the following settings (alternatives-from-evidence = true, alternatives-from-sampling = true, UTR = on, strand = both, minexonintronprob = 0.3, minmeanexonintronprob = 0.6, maxtracks = 2, softmasking = 1). Finally, high confidence gene models were defined as those fulfilling having 10 or more hits to the nr database at <i>e</i>-value &lt; 10<sup>−8</sup> and/or expressed with at least 100 unique counts (~0.18 reads per million mapped reads) between all RNAseq samples (calculated with HTSeq-0.6.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Anders, S., Pyl, P. T. &amp; Huber, W. HTSeq--a Python framework to work with high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics 31, 166–169 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1916">57</a></sup>). This dual filter excludes only genes that are neither clearly expressed nor homologous to known genes in other species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec24">Gene expression analysis</h3><p>Trimmed RNASeq reads were mapped as for gene calling above, except that the new gene models were provided to TopHat to facilitate mapping across identified splice junctions (-G gene_models.gff). Subsequently, unique counts were quantified using HTSeq-0.6.1 using default parameters expect for --stranded = no. Paired and single reads were counted separately and summed as fragments using cufflinks v2.2.1 using the -G setting. Differential gene expression analysis was performed with edgeR.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec25">Transposon composition</h3><p>Transposons were detected and classified by a homology search against the REdat_9.8_Eudicot section of the PGSB transposon library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Spannagl, M. et al. PGSB PlantsDB: updates to the database framework for comparative plant genome research. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, D1141–D1147 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1937">58</a></sup> which had been complemented beforehand by 974 non-redundant <i>C. campestris</i> specific full length LTR retrotransposons as described below to identify fragmented and partial copies. The program vmatch (<a href="http://www.vmatch.de">http://www.vmatch.de</a>), a fast and efficient matching tool, was used with the following parameters: identity≥70%, minimal hit length 75 bp, seedlength 12 bp (exact commandline: ­d ­p -l 75 ­identity 70 ­seedlength 12 ­exdrop 5). The vmatch output was filtered for redundant hits via a priority based approach, which assigns higher scoring matches first and either shortens (&lt;90% coverage and ≥50 bp rest length) or removes lower scoring overlaps to obtain an overlap free annotation. Full-length LTR retrotransposons elements were identified with LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinform. 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1951">59</a></sup>, which reported 7012 non-overlapping candidate sequences under the following parameter settings: “overlaps best -seed 30 -minlenltr 100 -maxlenltr 2000 -mindistltr 3000 -maxdistltr 25000 -similar 85 -mintsd 4 -maxtsd 20 -motif tgca -motifmis 1 -vic 60 -xdrop 5 -mat 2 -mis −2 -ins −3 -del −3”. All candidates were annotated for PfamA domains with hmmer3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Eddy, S. R. Accelerated profile HMM searches. PLoS Comput. Biol. 7, e1002195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1955">60</a></sup> (<a href="http://hmmer.org/">http://hmmer.org/</a>) and stringently filtered for false positives by the following criteria: (1) presence of at least one typical retrotransposon domain (RT, RH, INT, GAG), (2) removal of hybrids with inconsistent domain mixes e.g. RT, RH, INT, RT, RH, (3) absence of gene Pfam domains, (4) strand consistency between domains and primer binding site, (5) tandem repeat content below 25%, (6) long terminal repeat≤25 of the element length and (7) an <i>N</i> content &lt; 5%. The inner domain order was used for the subfamily classification into Ty3/<i>gypsy</i> (RT-RH-INT), Ty1/<i>copia</i> (INT-RT-RH) or unknown if either INT or RT were missing. The filtering steps led to a final set of 974 high confidence full-length LTR retrotransposons, which were clustered with a &gt;95% identity over &gt;95% mutual coverage to a non-redundant set of 969 elements.</p><p>LTR retrotransposons (8–20 kb) have the special structural property of LTRs (~1–2 kb) at both ends, which are identical upon insertion because only the 5′ end serves as a template. This feature enables a dating of the insertion event by comparing the divergence between the 5′ and 3′ LTRs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="SanMiguel, P., Gaut, B. S., Tikhonov, A., Nakajima, Y. &amp; Bennetzen, J. L. The paleontology of intergene retrotransposons of maize. Nat. Genet. 20, 43–45 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1979">61</a></sup>. A random mutation rate of 7*10<sup>−9</sup> was used to convert the sequence divergence into age<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Ossowski, S. et al. The rate and molecular spectrum of spontaneous mutations in Arabidopsis thaliana. Science 327, 92–94 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1985">62</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec26">Orthogroup and functional enrichment analysis</h3><p>Orthogroups were created with OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d35684e1997">63</a></sup> from proteins of primary transcripts of the <i>C</i>. <i>campestris</i> high-confidence set, <i>Ipomoea</i> nil, <i>Solanum</i> <i>lycopersicum</i>, <i>Amaranthus hypochondriacus</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Daucus carota</i>, <i>Mimulus guttatus</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>Sorghum bicolor</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, and <i>Dioscorea rotundata</i>. Sequences for additional species were obtained from Phytozome v12.0 (<a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov</a>), except I. nil from ftp://ftp.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genomes/Ipomoea_nil/, and <i>D. rotundata</i> from <a href="http://genome-e.ibrc.or.jp/home/bioinformatics-team/yam">http://genome-e.ibrc.or.jp/home/bioinformatics-team/yam</a>. Functional categories were assigned to each <i>A. thaliana</i> gene using Mercator. Fisher’s exact test was used to test for enrichment of functional categories (based on the function of all the <i>A. thaliana</i> genes in each orthogroup) in orthogroups without a <i>C. campestris</i> gene. Specifically, to characterize gene loss a comparison was performed between 0:1 (or more) and remaining orthogroups (<i>C. campestris</i>: each other dicot species; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig3">3c</a>). Complementarily, to characterize gene duplication, for orthogroups with at least one gene in every dicot species, a comparison was performed between orthogroups with a ratio of at least 2<i>n</i>:<i>n</i> (<i>C. campestris</i>: each other dicot species; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig3">3c</a>) and the rest. The false discovery rate was calculated according to the Benjamini and Yekutieli procedure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Yekutieli, D. The control of the false discovery rate in multiple testing under dependency. Ann. Stat. 29, 1165–1188 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d35684e2091">64</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec27">Synteny analysis</h3><p>Syntenic regions and tandem duplicates were identified with MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d35684e2103">65</a></sup> using stricter than default settings (<i>e</i>-value 10<sup>−20</sup>, maximum gaps 15, minimum size of collinear block 8). The protein BLAST results from Orthofinder were used as input. MCScanX was ran once on the individual species <i>C. campestris</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> to classify paralogs, and once with <i>C. campestris</i> and the high quality reference assemblies of <i>S. lycopersicum</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> together to determine cross-species ratios.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec28">Substitution rate analysis</h3><p>The synonymous substitution rate (d<i>S</i>) was calculated for 1:1 orthologs (other species: <i>C. campestris</i>) and paralogous pairs (exactly two genes in orthogroup for <i>C. campestris</i>). Protein alignment was performed with PRANK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Loytynoja, A. &amp; Goldman, N. An algorithm for progressive multiple alignment of sequences with insertions. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 10557–10562 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d35684e2145">66</a></sup>, converted to nucleotide alignment with pal2nal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D. &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequence alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W609–W612 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d35684e2149">67</a></sup>, and dS was calculated with codeml from PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d35684e2154">68</a></sup>. All of the above was orchestrated via the wrapper program phasePAML (<a href="https://github.com/janinamass/phasePAML">https://github.com/janinamass/phasePAML</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec29">Comparative analysis of gene losses across the plant kingdom</h3><p>A comprehensive search for gene losses in <i>C. campestris</i> was performed using <i>I. nil</i>, a non-parasitic plant within the same taxonomic family of Convolvulaceae, as reference. Protein sequences from <i>I. nil</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hoshino, A. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the Japanese morning glory Ipomoea nil. Nat. Commun. 7, 13295 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d35684e2181">14</a></sup> were tested against a comprehensive set of protein sequences from more than hundred Angiosperm species (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM6">4</a>) to select all proteins that were conserved within or beyond the order Solanales using a BLASTp search with score values &gt; 50. The 23,700 conserved candidate protein sets were analyzed for the presence of homologs in <i>Cuscuta</i> using our <i>C. campestris</i> protein models as well as the predicted proteins from the transcriptomes of <i>C. pentagona</i> and <i>C. suaveolens</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Ranjan, A. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the transcriptome of the parasitic weed Cuscuta pentagona identifies genes associated with plant parasitism. Plant Physiol. 166, 1186–1199 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d35684e2200">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Jiang, L. et al. Profiling mRNAs of two Cuscuta species reveals possible candidate transcripts shared by parasitic plants. PLoS ONE 8, e81389 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d35684e2203">69</a></sup>. The protein sets without significant <i>Cuscuta</i> sp. homologs were used to generate multiple sequence alignments. Hidden Markov Models (HMM) constructed from these multiple alignments were used to verify the absence of homologous <i>Cuscuta</i> sp. proteins. Finally, 1736 proteins from <i>I. nil</i> without significant <i>Cuscuta</i> sp. homologs were confirmed.</p><p>Gene presence or absence in 14 dicotyledonous angiosperms, two monocotyledonous angiosperms, one moss and two Chlorophyte species was also inferred for a selected set of proteins (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#Fig4">4a</a>) using a multistep BLASTp analysis. All amino acid sequences were derived from available whole-genome data sets, published without restrictions on Phytozome v12.0.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec30">Identification and verification of HGT candidates</h3><p>HGT candidates were identified using an “extended best hit” approach that allows eliminating false or dubious HGT results. First, BLASTp-analysis of all translated primary transcripts of the <i>C. campestris</i> high-confidence set was performed against the nr database with hit parameters limited to 25 hits per protein and an <i>e</i> value of 1e<sup>−5</sup>. The BLAST results for each protein were assigned to taxonomic bins based on the hit scores using the MEGAN v.6.0.0 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Huson, D. H. et al. MEGAN community edition-interactive exploration and analysis of large-scale microbiome sequencing data. PLoS Comput. Biol. 12, e1004957 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d35684e2242">70</a></sup> using the following parameters: minSupport = 1, minScore = 50, maxExpected = 0.01, minPercentIdentity = 0, topPercent = 40, lcaPercent = 100, minComplexity = 0, paired reads = false, useIdentityFilter = false. The topPercent value is a relative cutoff value that determines which bitscore, relative to the best-hit bitscore, any hit must have in order to be counted as part of the taxonomic bin containing the entire set (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">4</a>). <i>C. campestris</i> proteins that were assigned to orders other than Solanales, were considered potential HGT candidates and were further subjected to filtering to obtain a high confidence set. The filtering steps included the removal of all proteins with a best hit bitscore of 80 or lower, as lower values compromised the topPercent = 40% rule, and a removal of sequences identified as transposable or repetitive elements and retrovirus sequences. Also, all sequence alignments were inspected manually and cases, where a drop in bitscores was due to a short inhomologous insertion that led to two instead of one alignments with lower bitscores each, were discarded. All HGT candidates were analyzed using the gene tree—species tree reconciliation approach of Notung<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Stolzer, M. et al. Inferring duplications, losses, transfers and incomplete lineage sorting with nonbinary species trees. Bioinformatics 28, i409–i415 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d35684e2253">24</a></sup> to get further proof for transfer events. This approach relies on a comparison of gene trees with the corresponding species trees and the identification of deviating placements of the gene of interest in both trees. For the species trees, orthologous sequences for 350 randomly selected amino acid sequences from <i>C. campestris</i> were identified by BLASTp (<i>e</i>-value 10<sup>−5</sup>, max_target_seqs 1) in 41 plant species representing different branches of the phylogenetic tree. The minimum length of the random sequences was 200 amino acids. For each of these orthologous sequence sets a sequence alignment and a phylogenetic tree was generated using Clustal omega (auto) and FastTree 2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Price, M. N., Dehal, P. S. &amp; Arkin, A. P. FastTree 2--approximately maximum-likelihood trees for large alignments. PLoS ONE 5, e9490 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d35684e2265">71</a></sup>. The resulting trees were then analyzed by ASTRAL-II<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Mirarab, S. &amp; Warnow, T. ASTRAL-II: coalescent-based species tree estimation with many hundreds of taxa and thousands of genes. Bioinformatics 31, i44–i52 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d35684e2270">72</a></sup> to generate a highly supported species tree (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">5</a>). Some of the randomly picked polypeptides were not found in all taxa, leading to trees with “missing” branches in the trees. Only trees with at least 39 of the expected 42 branches were used. The analysis was repeated four times with similar results.</p><p>For the computation of HGT candidate gene trees, orthologous sequences of the 73 translated HGT candidates of <i>C. campestris</i> (7 albumin genes, 2 strictosidine synthase-like genes, and 64 novel candidates, see Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#MOESM1">4</a>) were identified by BLASTp (<i>e</i>-value 10<sup>−5</sup>). For each candidate, potential donor scenarios were reconstructed through reconciliation of the respective gene tree with the species tree using Notung 2.9<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Stolzer, M. et al. Inferring duplications, losses, transfers and incomplete lineage sorting with nonbinary species trees. Bioinformatics 28, i409–i415 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-04344-z#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d35684e2291">24</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec31">Data availability</h3><p>Raw reads, the assembled genome sequence and annotations are available from the European Nucleotide Archive (ENA) under accession number <a href="https://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/data/view/PRJEB19879">PRJEB19879</a> and from the plaBi database (<a href="http://plabipd.de/portal/cuscuta-campestris">http://plabipd.de/portal/cuscuta-campestris</a>). Sequence alignments are deposited in the Open Science Framework (<a href="https://osf.io/KQD9V/">https://osf.io/KQD9V/</a>) (<a href="https://doi.org/10.17605/OSF.IO/KQD9V">https://doi.org/10.17605/OSF.IO/KQD9V</a>).</p></div></div></section>

A.V., M.E.B., M.H.-W.S., and K.K. performed nucleic acid extractions, library preparations and sequencing. A.V., A.K.D., B.U., and A.B. performed the genome assembly and annotation. H.G. and K.F.X.M. performed the repetitive element analysis. R.S., A.K.D., B.U., J.H., K.F., and K.K. analyzed genome characteristics and gene loss. J.H., K.F., and K.K. performed HGT analysis.
